<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a8181ad8-beeb-444c-82ed-194890381a87'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

  <span id="freeText1730511833299077555" style="display:none"><b>The National Book Award finalist from the best-selling author of <i>Because of Winn-Dixie</i>—now in paperback</b><br /><br />Walking through the misty Florida woods one morning, twelve-year-old Rob Horton is stunned to encounter a tiger—a real-life, very large tiger—pacing back and forth in a cage. What's more, on the same extraordinary day, he meets Sistine Bailey, a girl who shows her feelings as readily as Rob hides his. As they learn to trust each other, and ultimately, to be friends, Rob and Sistine prove that some things—like memories, and heartaches, and tigers—can't be locked up forever.</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2553704276" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2553704276" />
    <a title="Mischenko" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1194517-mischenko"><img alt="Mischenko" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587209348p2/1194517.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2553704276?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mischenko" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mischenko" href="/user/show/1194517-mischenko">Mischenko</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1194517-mischenko?shelf=middle-grade-children-s">middle-grade-children-s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2553704276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2082805068499118277">At this point, I’m fairly certain there isn’t anything Kate DiCamillo can write that we won’t enjoy. This one wasn’t as good as the others we’ve read so far, but we still enjoyed it. It turned out to be a speedy read at just a smidge over 100 pages.<br/><br/>Rob Horton, a 12-year-old boy, lives with his dad at the Kentucky Star Motel. His mother (Caroline) recently passed away from cancer and he’s dealing with a lot of bottled up emotion from that, plus he deals with a blistering skin condition on his leg</span>
  <span id="freeText2082805068499118277" style="display:none">At this point, I’m fairly certain there isn’t anything Kate DiCamillo can write that we won’t enjoy. This one wasn’t as good as the others we’ve read so far, but we still enjoyed it. It turned out to be a speedy read at just a smidge over 100 pages.<br /><br />Rob Horton, a 12-year-old boy, lives with his dad at the Kentucky Star Motel. His mother (Caroline) recently passed away from cancer and he’s dealing with a lot of bottled up emotion from that, plus he deals with a blistering skin condition on his legs and bulling at school. He has unresolved problems with his dad because both of them are still mourning the loss of Caroline. While wondering in the woods behind the motel one day, he discovers a tiger locked up in a cage. Rob is fascinated by the tiger as it seems to have magically appeared.<br /><br /><i>Tyger Tyger, burning bright, <br />In the forests of the night; <br />What immortal hand or eye, <br />Could frame thy fearful symmetry?</i><br /><br />Meanwhile, at school, Rob meets the new girl (Sistine) and they begin to develop a beautiful friendship. Sistine has her own problems she’s dealing with and she’s just flat out angry about everything. She’s quite aggressive and outspoken. She encourages Rob to talk about his mom and to deal with his emotions.<br /><br /><i>“I don’t want to see your stupid tiger!” she shouted. ” I don’t care about it. You don’t know how to talk to people. I told you about my father and my mother and Bridgette, and you didn’t say anything”…”Keep your stupid secrets!” she shouted. ” Keep your stupid tiger, too. I don’t care.”</i><br /><br />Surprisingly, Rob eventually finds that it’s not so complicated to open up to her. He shows Sistine the tiger and after she meets the tiger, she has her own opinions on what they should do about it. She want’s Rob to follow through on her plan, but Rob isn’t sure what to do. Will he make the right decision?<br /><br />The story started off a little boring at first, but then as I read on and more characters came in, along with their individual backgrounds, I simply couldn’t put the book down. I think my favorite character in the book turned out to be Willie May–she was so wise and warmhearted. I enjoyed all the characters though–sympathizing with each one–similar to how I felt reading Because of Winn-Dixie.<br /><br />With this being a coming of age story that deals with themes of friendship, grief, and loss, I definitely had some tears. I didn’t find the book over-predictable at all and was quite surprised with the end. My two 4th grade children didn’t seem to enjoy this one as much as the others by this author we’ve recently read, but they were still able to hang in throughout. I have no doubt they’ll enjoy reading it again as they get a little older.<br /><br />This is another one of Kate DiCamillo’s books that’s wonderfully written with strong character development and symbolism. I’m happy to have this one added to our collection.<br /><br />4****<br /><br />You can read this review on my blog @ <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://readrantrockandroll.com/2018/10/18/book-review-the-tiger-rising-by-kate-dicamillo-bookreview-katedicamillo/">https://readrantrockandroll.com/2018/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2082805068499118277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_127973825" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/127973825" />
    <a title="The Rusty Key" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4464559-the-rusty-key"><img alt="The Rusty Key" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1288102499p2/4464559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/127973825?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The Rusty Key" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The Rusty Key" href="/user/show/4464559-the-rusty-key">The Rusty Key</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer127973825" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4097235215917651045"> Reviewed by Rusty Key Writer: Jordan B. Nielsen<br/><br/>Recommended for: EVERYONE aged twelve and up, both boys and girls. There are some adult themes, and mild, but poignant violence, which will probably be more affecting to those over twenty than the younger set, who might not feel its full force.<br/><br/>One Word Summary: Radiant<br/><br/>Oh Kate, you’ve done it again. The words ‘Kate DiCamillo’ are becoming more than just a name, but a state of being. If you’re feeling a little ‘Kate DiCamillo’, chances are you’re a b</span>
  <span id="freeText4097235215917651045" style="display:none"> Reviewed by Rusty Key Writer: Jordan B. Nielsen<br /><br />Recommended for: EVERYONE aged twelve and up, both boys and girls. There are some adult themes, and mild, but poignant violence, which will probably be more affecting to those over twenty than the younger set, who might not feel its full force.<br /><br />One Word Summary: Radiant<br /><br />Oh Kate, you’ve done it again. The words ‘Kate DiCamillo’ are becoming more than just a name, but a state of being. If you’re feeling a little ‘Kate DiCamillo’, chances are you’re a bit weepy and nostalgic, wishing to just lie on your bed all day and watch the wind in the trees, contemplating things lost. Her prior work, ‘The Miraculous Journey of Edward Tulane’ became one of my favorite books before I’d even finished it or mopped myself up out of the puddle of my own hysterical tears. So I entered The Tiger Rising with a ready tissue box, the bedroom door closed, and the certainty that my next few days would be spent in a wistful, heart-rendered cloud. I was not disappointed. <br /><br />On his program Real Time, Bill Maher once condemned a nation who believes Harry Potter to be a great work of literature as a bunch of morons. I won’t get on my soap box right now about the greatness of Harry, but The Tiger Rising is exactly the kind of work that I would point to to as proof that anyone who thinks that books meant for children can never match the intellectual complexity of a work written for adults, completely and utterly wrong. <br /><br /> Set in an overcast and swampy Florida, in that un-nameable book-time of before now and after World War II, The Tiger Rising follows Rob, a sixth grade boy, whose mother has recently died of cancer, now living in a motel with his quietly grief-paralyzed father. Rob is an outcast at school, bullied by thugs, overlooked by adults, and teased for a skin condition that has resulted from his own suppressed grief. His misunderstood rash, however irritating, proves to be his savior as he’s sent home from school indefinitely, for fear of spreading it to his fellow classmates, who are oh-so-deserving of something virulent. <br /><br />And then, inexplicably, there is a Tiger. In the woods behind the motel Rob finds the cage, the great orange beauty stalking back and forth in its tiny enclosure, alone and breathtakingly out of place. Rob is enthralled, a sense of wonderment and elation brought back to his life that was stuffed down into his “suitcase of not-thoughts” with the loss of his mother. Rob’s only friend, Sistine, a new girl in town, full of outrage and her own personal loss, is brought in on the secret of the Tiger. Sistine wants to free it, Rob can’t bare to see it go. <br /><br />The story is heavy with metaphor, and if I were forced at gunpoint to name a flaw of the book, I might say the metaphors are occasionally a little too heavy, but I think only cynical, well-read adults would sense that. There are elements and plot turns to this story that are familiar, perhaps even predictable, but the reason why I’m untroubled by this is that the setting, prose, and characters are all so cleanly written and sharply real that the work stands on its own. It’s the DiCamillo style in full force: so frank and pure that you could never call it sentimental, even though it’s rich with sentiment. The very slimness of the book in your hands shouts that what you hold is like a comet in the night, here and then gone, arresting and haunting. So slim that I won’t go further into the plot, as there is not much more to tell without taking away the joy of its discovery. An outstanding read. <br /><br /><br />For more reviews from The Rusty Key, visit us at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.therustykey.com">www.therustykey.com</a><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4097235215917651045" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2329963370" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2329963370?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henry Martin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henry Martin" href="/user/show/14734360-henry-martin">Henry Martin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14734360-henry-martin?shelf=children-s-books">children-s-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14734360-henry-martin?shelf=permanent-collection">permanent-collection</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2329963370" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4917706476359050160">I have been a huge fan of Kate DiCamillo's writing for a long time, often comparing some aspects of her children's books to literary fiction. This book, while not her best, did not disappoint. <br/><br/>As usual in DiCamillo's longer works, there is a dark side to this tale of a boy whose mother recently passed away, whose father works as a maintenance man at a run-down motel, and whose inner pain is so strong that it causes his legs to sprout blisters. <br/><br/>DiCamillo takes this boy, Rob, and makes me want to </span>
  <span id="freeText4917706476359050160" style="display:none">I have been a huge fan of Kate DiCamillo's writing for a long time, often comparing some aspects of her children's books to literary fiction. This book, while not her best, did not disappoint. <br /><br />As usual in DiCamillo's longer works, there is a dark side to this tale of a boy whose mother recently passed away, whose father works as a maintenance man at a run-down motel, and whose inner pain is so strong that it causes his legs to sprout blisters. <br /><br />DiCamillo takes this boy, Rob, and makes me want to care about him, makes me want to understand his pain, and makes me long to find a way to help him open up his 'suitcase' where he keeps all of his feelings shut tight. <br /><br />Then along comes Sistine. A beautiful little girls whose father ran away with his secretary. She also harbors a great deal of pain and anger, but unlike Rob, she channels it outward. <br /><br />Throw the two of them together, add a sleazy motel owner, a wise cleaning lady, and a tiger locked in a cage in the woods behind the motel, and you have a recipe for a tale that is engaging not only to young readers but adults as well. (Well, those adults who still know how to nourish the little bit of the inner child the world did not steal from us.) <br /><br />Typical of DiCamillo, the character depth is amazing, the interactions realistic, and the writing superb. <br /><br />And with the tiger burning bright, Rob and Sistine may just find a way to deal with what injustices life served them. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4917706476359050160" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3526055157512003429">I read this book while at camp. It wasn't very exciting, or with much of a climax. Let me give you a plot summary- There is a tiger in town, this girl likes it, and so does this boy. They want to let it out, but the boy's father works for the man who is holding the tiger captive.   <span id="freeText3526055157512003429" style="display:none">I read this book while at camp. It wasn't very exciting, or with much of a climax. Let me give you a plot summary- There is a tiger in town, this girl likes it, and so does this boy. They want to let it out, but the boy's father works for the man who is holding the tiger captive. SO they finally let the tiger go, and guess what, the dad shoots it. He claims a loose tiger would've been dangerous.... The ending leaves you with a "so what" effect, leaving you to wonder what you gained from the book. I didn't gain much, for sure. The moral was confusing, and the poor boy with a dead mom in the story doesn't change much throughout the whole story, which might hae been gratifying in itself. The girl remains a snob the whole book, and well, I'm left with the "so what" thing, because what's the point again?</span>

<div id="review_183285450" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/183285450" />
    <a title="Cathy Costello" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5734822-cathy-costello"><img alt="Cathy Costello" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/183285450?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cathy Costello" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cathy Costello" href="/user/show/5734822-cathy-costello">Cathy Costello</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer183285450" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1597507194488670702">I’ve read several of Kate DiCamillo’s novels, and I use two of them with my students. I was hoping that The Rising Tiger would be one that I could add to my repertoire, but I’m afraid that I really didn’t care for it. I found the characters to be underdeveloped. I know that Rob and his father have suffered through a tragedy, but I didn’t learn much more about them beyond that. As a result, I didn’t really bond with them. I felt the same way about Sistine. She’s a flashy character in the way she </span>
  <span id="freeText1597507194488670702" style="display:none">I’ve read several of Kate DiCamillo’s novels, and I use two of them with my students. I was hoping that The Rising Tiger would be one that I could add to my repertoire, but I’m afraid that I really didn’t care for it. I found the characters to be underdeveloped. I know that Rob and his father have suffered through a tragedy, but I didn’t learn much more about them beyond that. As a result, I didn’t really bond with them. I felt the same way about Sistine. She’s a flashy character in the way she dresses and acts, but without more development she comes across as more spoiled than sympathetic. I understand what the tiger was supposed to represent, but it was dealt with so superficially that I think the purpose of its presence would be lost to most ten year olds. <br /><br />	I did like the character of Willie May. She came across as genuine and endearing. I also liked Rob’s suitcase of not-thought about things. I thought that was a powerful metaphor, and it was the only thing I really connected with in the story. <br /><br />	I read this novel with great expectations, but was left wanting in the end. DiCamillo frequently uses tragedy in her stories but generally as a starting off point to begin her characters’ journeys. In this novel, I felt like she relied too heavily on tragedy to be the plot. As a result, her character didn’t develop, and I never really bonded with them. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1597507194488670702" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer170349047" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11636408142376683828"><i>The Tiger Rising</i> is the story of two very sad and broken children, and how they are saved by a tiger, but not at all in the way you would expect. This story broke my heart. There's something about the way DiCamillo writes that touches my deepest emotions. I don't know if I can explain how or why, but it's as if I feel the stories, instead of reading them. It's some strange retro-childhood cathartic experience that makes me want to cry myself clean, as if my child-tears could wash away all the st</span>
  <span id="freeText11636408142376683828" style="display:none"><i>The Tiger Rising</i> is the story of two very sad and broken children, and how they are saved by a tiger, but not at all in the way you would expect. This story broke my heart. There's something about the way DiCamillo writes that touches my deepest emotions. I don't know if I can explain how or why, but it's as if I feel the stories, instead of reading them. It's some strange retro-childhood cathartic experience that makes me want to cry myself clean, as if my child-tears could wash away all the stains of jaded adulthood.</span>

It was a bit depressing throughout, with tinged of hope around the edges. I've read it four time as a read-aloud for 4th graders, and it's getting better each time. A lot of different ways to read this, and it's like a non-stinky onion with lots of layers.</span>

<div id="review_3118280677" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3118280677" />
    <a title="Cynthia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10560051-cynthia"><img alt="Cynthia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542841773p2/10560051.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3118280677?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cynthia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cynthia" href="/user/show/10560051-cynthia">Cynthia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3118280677" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9143569649315335488">“Sadness,” said Willie May, closing her eyes and nodding her head. “You keep all that sadness down low, in your legs. You not letting it get up to your heart, where it belongs. You got to let that sadness rise on up.” <br/>********************************************************<br/>I wish Kate DiCamillo’s books had existed when I was a child. I read some fun books as a kid but not many that would stick with me throughout my lifetime. Kate DiCamillo would have satisfied the kid version of me who was looki</span>
  <span id="freeText9143569649315335488" style="display:none">“Sadness,” said Willie May, closing her eyes and nodding her head. “You keep all that sadness down low, in your legs. You not letting it get up to your heart, where it belongs. You got to let that sadness rise on up.” <br />********************************************************<br />I wish Kate DiCamillo’s books had existed when I was a child. I read some fun books as a kid but not many that would stick with me throughout my lifetime. Kate DiCamillo would have satisfied the kid version of me who was looking for something a little more in a story. But thank goodness, because of my daughter’s love for the author, I’ve had the chance to experience so many of DiCamillo’s deeply moving stories as an adult.<br /><br />The Tiger Rising tells the tale of Rob and Sistine, two children with very different backgrounds who both carry their own confusing forms of grief. Through each other, a hotel housekeeper, and a caged tiger, they begin to learn that wild things, like painful emotions, need to be set free. <br /><br />I enjoyed the story and the wisdom it offered. If I were still a child, I would have likely been fully satisfied. As an adult, I found myself wanting a bit more. There was so much depth but it felt like it all came to a close too quickly. At any rate, I highly recommend this precious book to everyone. It has meaningful takeaways for children and adults alike.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9143569649315335488" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline D&#39;cruz " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline D&#39;cruz " href="/user/show/95870560-caroline-d-cruz">Caroline D&#39;cruz </a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3291406826" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer902559649072204027">This is the first book I have read by Kate DiCamillo. Took to reading this one for my Nephew as it's an ongoing novel read at his school. Though the book is well written with each character played out well, I just feel that the book fell a bit heavy on emotion in the end for a children's book. I just feel that from a child point of veiw it's a bit hard to digest all that lose and grief. <br/><br/>On a lighter note the book also talks of a very loving bond between a father and son after the death of the bo</span>
  <span id="freeText902559649072204027" style="display:none">This is the first book I have read by Kate DiCamillo. Took to reading this one for my Nephew as it's an ongoing novel read at his school. Though the book is well written with each character played out well, I just feel that the book fell a bit heavy on emotion in the end for a children's book. I just feel that from a child point of veiw it's a bit hard to digest all that lose and grief. <br /><br />On a lighter note the book also talks of a very loving bond between a father and son after the death of the boys (Rob's) mother, of a budding friendship and trust between to twelve year old children and the love to set an animal who is caged up. <br /><br /><br />The Tiger Rising is the story of twelve-year-old Rob Horton who is stunned to encounter a tiger—a real-life, very large tiger—pacing back and forth in a cage. What's more, on the same extraordinary day, he meets Sistine Bailey, a girl who shows her feelings as readily as Rob hides his. As they learn to trust each other, and ultimately, to be friends, Rob and Sistine prove that some things—like memories, and heartaches, and tigers—can't be locked up forever.<br /><br />All in all a good story.👍🏻</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3047749573" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3047749573" />
    <a title="Sarah Booth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3524795-sarah-booth"><img alt="Sarah Booth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566943331p2/3524795.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3047749573?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah Booth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah Booth" href="/user/show/3524795-sarah-booth">Sarah Booth</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3047749573&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3047749573" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5565170859082382349">How do you rate something like this? It's an amazing and excellent story that broke my heart. It's an incredibly well done story and destined to be a classic, but I hate sad stories that make me cry. As the Belcher children learn from reading children's books on Bob's Burgers, children's books never end well for the animals. I happen to prefer more magical unrealistic stories where there is happy riding off into the sunset and no one gets it because they are surrounded by ugly ignorant people. T</span>
  <span id="freeText5565170859082382349" style="display:none">How do you rate something like this? It's an amazing and excellent story that broke my heart. It's an incredibly well done story and destined to be a classic, but I hate sad stories that make me cry. As the Belcher children learn from reading children's books on Bob's Burgers, children's books never end well for the animals. I happen to prefer more magical unrealistic stories where there is happy riding off into the sunset and no one gets it because they are surrounded by ugly ignorant people. The real world has too much beauty destroyed by said forces of ignorance which seem to have the upper hand these days. While it's an important lesson for children to learn about the real world, stressed out middle aged women have already learned that difficult lesson; either that or they are the problem and are the Beauchamps of the world and the story would be lost on them. <br />So a brilliant and excellent story which is a wonderful teaching tool, but bad escapism-type reading. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5565170859082382349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer12027695" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16571572545411365990">Wow. I'm quite speechless. This book is simply such an amazing book. Although the storyline particularly for me was a bit boring, but the meaning and the metaphors from this book were extraordinary! I didn't like how the author killed the tiger at the end but I understand why she did, it made it more powerful. I for one, did not clearly understand this meaning... a bit confusing for me. Another thing that I thought would happen was if the rash did go away after he let out his sadness. The book d</span>
  <span id="freeText16571572545411365990" style="display:none">Wow. I'm quite speechless. This book is simply such an amazing book. Although the storyline particularly for me was a bit boring, but the meaning and the metaphors from this book were extraordinary! I didn't like how the author killed the tiger at the end but I understand why she did, it made it more powerful. I for one, did not clearly understand this meaning... a bit confusing for me. Another thing that I thought would happen was if the rash did go away after he let out his sadness. The book didn't mention that but it would be awesome if it did. Though it may seem to fictiony, it still would have been nice. The tiger itself was not really as magical as I had thought it was supposed to be, it was just another... small subject in my view. It had seem much too unreal much less magical. I encourage my friends to read this book because the meaning is powerful and how the author expresses each event and feeling is simply amazing besides the few minor opinions listed above.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_412984817" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Frances" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/752906-frances"><img alt="Frances" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325710156p2/752906.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/412984817?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Frances" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Frances" href="/user/show/752906-frances">Frances</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/752906-frances?shelf=children-s">children-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/752906-frances?shelf=realistic-fiction">realistic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/752906-frances?shelf=read-in-2009">read-in-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer412984817" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17785631511468234547">**spoilers**<br/><br/>I should preface this by saying that I usually love Kate DiCamillo. Although the book was very short, the characters were well developed. Here's the kicker for me: it read like a school book. Let's all talk about the tiger's death and whether it had to happen. Should Rob have done anything differently? etc. The setting is a little strange, almost like the book is set in a bubble. I don't mind this-- it gives it a timeless quality that many classic children's books have-- but it makes</span>
  <span id="freeText17785631511468234547" style="display:none">**spoilers**<br /><br />I should preface this by saying that I usually love Kate DiCamillo. Although the book was very short, the characters were well developed. Here's the kicker for me: it read like a school book. Let's all talk about the tiger's death and whether it had to happen. Should Rob have done anything differently? etc. The setting is a little strange, almost like the book is set in a bubble. I don't mind this-- it gives it a timeless quality that many classic children's books have-- but it makes the characters a little harder to relate to. I also wanted a little more exposition when the tiger is shot, or just a little more time spent with Sistine and Willie May or Rob's father before it happens. The gaps, however, do lend even more opportunities for class discussion points.... which could be good if that's what you're looking for. It didn't grab me, but it is unique and powerful. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17785631511468234547" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2907830407" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2907830407" />
    <a title="Abigail" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/87835420-abigail"><img alt="Abigail" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539121384p2/87835420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2907830407?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abigail" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abigail" href="/user/show/87835420-abigail">Abigail</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780763652708" href="/book/show/9327146-the-tiger-rising">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Readers Looking for Children's Stories About Grief, Family &amp; Friendship</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=childrens-fiction">childrens-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=death-grieving">death-grieving</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=bullying">bullying</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=florida">florida</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2907830407" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6928452102399200903">Like her earlier work, <u>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/357664.Because_of_Winn_Dixie' title='Because of Winn-Dixie by Kate DiCamillo' rel='nofollow noopener'>Because of Winn-Dixie</a>
</u>, which was awarded a Newbery Honor in 2001, this slim children's novel is set in Florida, and follows the story of a young person coping with an absent mother and a distant father. In <u>The Tiger Rising</u>, Rob Horton, still reeling from the death of his mother, and recently moved to a new town, is confronted with bullying at his new school. His father, also deeply bereaved, has made it very clear that Rob is not to express his grief, not to cry. Then </span>
  <span id="freeText6928452102399200903" style="display:none">Like her earlier work, <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/357664.Because_of_Winn_Dixie" title="Because of Winn-Dixie by Kate DiCamillo" rel="nofollow noopener">Because of Winn-Dixie</a>
</u>, which was awarded a Newbery Honor in 2001, this slim children's novel is set in Florida, and follows the story of a young person coping with an absent mother and a distant father. In <u>The Tiger Rising</u>, Rob Horton, still reeling from the death of his mother, and recently moved to a new town, is confronted with bullying at his new school. His father, also deeply bereaved, has made it very clear that Rob is not to express his grief, not to cry. Then two extraordinary things happen: Rob comes across a caged tiger in the woods near the hotel where he is living, and he meets a feisty new girl, Sistine (named for the chapel), who doesn't believe in reserve. Will Rob free the tiger, as Sistine insists they must... and if he does, what will happen?<br /><br />A moving book, one which chronicles one boy's journey from silent grief through cathartic rage, and then his entry into gradual healing, <u>The Tiger Rising</u> reminded me (as already noted) of <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/357664.Because_of_Winn_Dixie" title="Because of Winn-Dixie by Kate DiCamillo" rel="nofollow noopener">Because of Winn-Dixie</a>
</u>. In that other work, the adoption of a stray dog leads to an opening of the relationship between a child and her father. Here, the finding and eventual <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[killing <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> of a tiger leads to a moment of dramatic confrontation, in which Rob finally expresses himself to his father, opening the floodgates of both of their griefs. Although a brief book, I liked the characterization here. I like that Rob's father is depicted as deeply flawed - he hits Rob (only once that the narrative reveals), and doesn't really know how to take care of him - but also deeply loving. I also liked the character of Willie May, whose humor and humanity come across very well. Recommended to anyone looking for children's stories about grief, family, and friendship.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6928452102399200903" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3182258463" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3182258463" />
    <a title="Beth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4063913-beth"><img alt="Beth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351396026p2/4063913.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3182258463?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth" href="/user/show/4063913-beth">Beth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780763680879" href="/book/show/25241615-the-tiger-rising">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4063913-beth?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3182258463" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15845192092310534999">This is so deliberate with metaphor that it’s almost ponderous with it. It has its moments, but it isn’t long enough to make it out from under that weight. <br/><br/>Bonus points for a literal Chekhov’s gun, though: the crashing end to the fantasy element in this book made me wish for something less metaphorical about grief, because there was something so raw and empty about it. That distance makes me wonder if maybe this was DiCamillo’s suitcase - a novel version reflecting the characters themselves.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3320485011" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18072403818016788157"><b>3.5 Stars</b><br/><br/>I read this with my nine-year-old son, and he liked it better than <i> Because of Winn Dixie</i>, which he read with his class in school. I love seeing him develop his reading tastes! The writing in this book is beautiful, and even though I found the extended metaphor to be a bit obvious or "on the nose," I think for the targeted audience, it is perfect. Highly recommended to read with the 8-12 kiddos!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1342729187" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14526107115349400951">WOOHOO SPOILER FREE<br/>Of course I am going to give this five stars, it's by my favorite author. But if I could, I might have given it a 4. 8, just because it might have been my least favorite of her books. <br/><br/>What I really like about this story is that there is a lot of character development in this book, with very important life lessons. <br/><br/>I also related to Rob in away I've never related to a character before: his itchy legs. My entire life I have never been able to stop the scratching. No medicine, no</span>
  <span id="freeText14526107115349400951" style="display:none">WOOHOO SPOILER FREE<br />Of course I am going to give this five stars, it's by my favorite author. But if I could, I might have given it a 4. 8, just because it might have been my least favorite of her books. <br /><br />What I really like about this story is that there is a lot of character development in this book, with very important life lessons. <br /><br />I also related to Rob in away I've never related to a character before: his itchy legs. My entire life I have never been able to stop the scratching. No medicine, no pep talk has ever made me stop. I just react a little differently to mosquito's than other people, it itches more and my skin gets tender around the bite. I've never read of a boy who had this problem like I did, and it made me comfortable while reading. <br />"Don't let them judge you, it'll go away soon, " I kept telling him. <br /><br />There were so few problems with this book I won't even write them down, just read it for yourself! <br /><br /> Kate captured your heart again, Leah, you should be happy. <br /><br />Great story, beautiful writing as always! (that's your que to pick it up and start reading) <br />P. S. I wouldn't give this to a child under the age of ten, as there is a small mention of an affair and cigarette use.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/430201410" />
    <a title="Hilda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1562980-hilda"><img alt="Hilda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542860759p2/1562980.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hilda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hilda" href="/user/show/1562980-hilda">Hilda</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer14296669560105329109"><b>It's hard for me to talk about your mama. I wouldn't never have believed that I could miss somebody the way I miss her. Saying her name pains me.         <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2585188198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9799975172579018033">setting: Kentucky Star Motel which is in rural Florida<br/><br/>This is a short story. True, it's 116 pages, but there aren't many words per page, and the story reads quickly and wraps up neatly, without 'wasting' words on tangents. AND, the school is populated by bullies. I'm not a fan of short stories, and I don't like reading about cruelty.<br/><br/>That said, the story moves along quickly and keeps the readers' attention while dealing with tough topics (bullies, death, divorce, life lessons). Both kids are ende</span>
  <span id="freeText9799975172579018033" style="display:none">setting: Kentucky Star Motel which is in rural Florida<br /><br />This is a short story. True, it's 116 pages, but there aren't many words per page, and the story reads quickly and wraps up neatly, without 'wasting' words on tangents. AND, the school is populated by bullies. I'm not a fan of short stories, and I don't like reading about cruelty.<br /><br />That said, the story moves along quickly and keeps the readers' attention while dealing with tough topics (bullies, death, divorce, life lessons). Both kids are endearing; I wanted to bring them home with me and help them heal. Their feelings, like the tiger itself, can't be caged in forever.<br /><br />I'd like to hear kids' thoughts about the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9799975172579018033" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_817982'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/817982.Stone_Fox"><img alt="Stone Fox" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1348701872l/817982._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZBs8SAaTqHvIOVbuVZZdgioBcFImHJXzv3rBYWrQzOmNdSUnfJ4bfR4MmSzU/VlWVwu8QUJDEKvBqPqS12bvMw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8199329547569894226">Kate DiCamillo, the newly named National Ambassador for Young People’s Literature for 2014–2015, says about stories, “When we read together, we connect. Together, we see the world. Together, we see one another.” Born in Philadelphia, the author lives in Minneapolis, where she faithfully writes two pages a day, five days a week.<br/><br/> Kate DiCamillo's own journey is something of a dream come true. After </span>
  <span id="freeText8199329547569894226" style="display:none">Kate DiCamillo, the newly named National Ambassador for Young People’s Literature for 2014–2015, says about stories, “When we read together, we connect. Together, we see the world. Together, we see one another.” Born in Philadelphia, the author lives in Minneapolis, where she faithfully writes two pages a day, five days a week.<br /><br /> Kate DiCamillo's own journey is something of a dream come true. After moving to Minnesota from Florida in her twenties, homesickness and a bitter winter helped inspire Because of Winn-Dixie - her first published novel, which, remarkably, became a runaway bestseller and snapped up a Newbery Honor. "After the Newbery committee called me, I spent the whole day walking into walls," she says. "I was stunned. And very, very happy."<br /><br />Her second novel, The Tiger Rising, went on to become a National Book Award Finalist. Since then, the master storyteller has written for a wide range of ages, including two comical early-chapter-book series - Mercy Watson, which stars a "porcine wonder" with an obsession for buttered toast, and Bink &amp; Gollie, which celebrates the tall and short of a marvelous friendship - as well as a luminous holiday picture book, Great Joy.<br /><br />Her latest novel, Flora &amp; Ulysses: The Illuminated Adventures, won the 2014 Newbery Medal. It was released in fall 2013 to great acclaim, including five starred reviews, and was an instant New York Times bestseller. Flora &amp; Ulysses is a laugh-out-loud story filled with eccentric, endearing characters and featuring an exciting new format - a novel interspersed with comic-style graphic sequences and full-page illustrations, all rendered in black and white by up-and-coming artist K. G. Campbell. It was a 2013 Parents' Choice Gold Award Winner and was chosen by Amazon, Publishers Weekly, Kirkus Reviews, and Common Sense Media as a Best Book of the Year.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8199329547569894226" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
